Napoleon refused to <a href="/wiki/Manumission" title="Manumission">manumit</a> the Russian <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serfs</a> because of concerns this might provoke a reaction in his army's rear. The serfs later committed atrocities against French soldiers during France's retreat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Russians avoided Napoleon's objective of a decisive engagement and instead retreated deeper into Russia. A brief attempt at resistance was made at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Smolensk_(1812)" title="Battle of Smolensk (1812)">Smolensk</a> in August; the Russians were defeated in a series of battles, and Napoleon resumed his advance. The Russians again avoided battle, although in a few cases this was only achieved because Napoleon uncharacteristically hesitated to attack when the opportunity arose. Owing to the Russian army's <a href="/wiki/Scorched_earth" title="Scorched earth">scorched earth</a> tactics, the French found it increasingly difficult to forage food for themselves and their horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[188]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Russians eventually offered battle outside Moscow on 7 September: the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Borodino" title="Battle of Borodino">Battle of Borodino</a> resulted in approximately 44,000 Russian and 35,000 French dead, wounded or captured, and may have been the bloodiest day of battle in history up to that point in time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[189]</a></sup> Although the French had won, the Russian army had accepted, and withstood, the major battle Napoleon had hoped would be decisive. Napoleon's own account was: "The most terrible of all my battles was the one before Moscow. The French showed themselves to be worthy of victory, but the Russians showed themselves worthy of being invincible".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Russian army withdrew and retreated past Moscow. Napoleon entered the city, assuming its fall would end the war and Alexander would negotiate peace. However, on orders of the city's governor <a href="/wiki/Fyodor_Rostopchin" title="Fyodor Rostopchin">Feodor Rostopchin</a>, rather than capitulation, Moscow was burned. After five weeks, Napoleon and his army left. In early November Napoleon became concerned about the loss of control back in France after the <a href="/wiki/Malet_coup_of_1812" title="Malet coup of 1812">Malet coup of 1812</a>. His army walked through snow up to their knees, and nearly 10,000 men and horses froze to death on the night of 8/9 November alone. After the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Berezina" title="Battle of Berezina">Battle of Berezina</a> Napoleon managed to escape but had to abandon much of the remaining artillery and baggage train. On 5 December, shortly before arriving in Vilnius, Napoleon left the army in a sledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[191]</a></sup>
</p><p>The French suffered in the course of a ruinous retreat, including from the harshness of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Winter" title="Russian Winter">Russian Winter</a>. The Armée had begun as over 400,000 frontline troops, with fewer than 40,000 crossing the <a href="/wiki/Berezina" title="Berezina">Berezina River</a> in November 1812.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[192]</a></sup> The Russians had lost 150,000 soldiers in battle and hundreds of thousands of civilians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[193]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="War_of_the_Sixth_Coalition">War of the Sixth Coalition</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Sixth_Coalition" title="War of the Sixth Coalition">War of the Sixth Coalition</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Montfort_-_Adieux_de_Napoleon_a_la_Garde_imperiale.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1464" data-file-width="1967" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Montfort_-_Adieux_de_Napoleon_a_la_Garde_imperiale.jpg/220px-Montfort_-_Adieux_de_Napoleon_a_la_Garde_imperiale.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Montfort_-_Adieux_de_Napoleon_a_la_Garde_imperiale.jpg/330px-Montfort_-_Adieux_de_Napoleon_a_la_Garde_imperiale.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Montfort_-_Adieux_de_Napoleon_a_la_Garde_imperiale.jpg/440px-Montfort_-_Adieux_de_Napoleon_a_la_Garde_imperiale.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Montfort_-_Adieux_de_Napoleon_a_la_Garde_imperiale.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Napoleon's farewell to his Imperial Guard, 20 April 1814</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Antoine-Alphonse_Montfort" title="Antoine-Alphonse Montfort">Antoine-Alphonse Montfort</a></div></div></div>
<p>There was a lull in fighting over the winter of 1812–13 while both the Russians and the French rebuilt their forces; Napoleon was able to field 350,000 troops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[194]</a></sup> Heartened by France's loss in Russia, Prussia joined with Austria, Sweden, Russia, Great Britain, Spain, and Portugal in a new coalition. Napoleon assumed command in Germany and inflicted a series of defeats on the Coalition culminating in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dresden" title="Battle of Dresden">Battle of Dresden</a> in August 1813.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite these successes, the numbers continued to mount against Napoleon, and the French army was pinned down by a force twice its size and lost at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Leipzig" title="Battle of Leipzig">Battle of Leipzig</a>. This was by far the largest battle of the Napoleonic Wars and cost more than 90,000 casualties in total.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Allies offered peace terms in the <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_proposals" title="Frankfurt proposals">Frankfurt proposals</a> in November 1813. Napoleon would remain as Emperor of the French, but it would be reduced to its "natural frontiers". That meant that France could retain control of Belgium, Savoy and the Rhineland (the west bank of the Rhine River), while giving up control of all the rest, including all of Spain and the Netherlands, and most of Italy and Germany. Metternich told Napoleon these were the best terms the Allies were likely to offer; after further victories, the terms would be harsher and harsher. Metternich's motivation was to maintain France as a balance against Russian threats while ending the highly destabilizing series of wars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-J._P._Riley_2013_206_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-J._P._Riley_2013_206-205">[197]</a></sup>
</p><p>Napoleon, expecting to win the war, delayed too long and lost this opportunity; by December the Allies had withdrawn the offer. When his back was to the wall in 1814 he tried to reopen peace negotiations on the basis of accepting the Frankfurt proposals. The Allies now had new, harsher terms that included the retreat of France to its 1791 boundaries, which meant the loss of Belgium. Napoleon would remain Emperor, however, he rejected the term. The British wanted Napoleon permanently removed, and they prevailed, but Napoleon adamantly refused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-J._P._Riley_2013_206_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-J._P._Riley_2013_206-205">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[198]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DelarocheNapoleon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="602" decoding="async" height="292" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/DelarocheNapoleon.jpg/220px-DelarocheNapoleon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/DelarocheNapoleon.jpg/330px-DelarocheNapoleon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/DelarocheNapoleon.jpg/440px-DelarocheNapoleon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DelarocheNapoleon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Napoleon after his abdication in Fontainebleau, 4 April 1814, by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Delaroche" title="Paul Delaroche">Paul Delaroche</a></div></div></div>
<p>Napoleon withdrew into France, his army reduced to 70,000 soldiers and little cavalry; he faced more than three times as many Allied troops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[199]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Bonaparte" title="Joseph Bonaparte">Joseph Bonaparte</a>, Napoleon's older brother, abdicated as king of Spain on 13 December 1813 and assumed the title of lieutenant general to save the collapsing empire. The French were surrounded: British armies pressed from the south, and other Coalition forces positioned to attack from the German states. By the middle of January 1814, the Coalition had already entered France's borders and launched a two-pronged attack on Paris, with Prussia entering from the north, and Austria from the East, marching out of the capitulated Swiss confederation. The French Empire, however, would not go down so easily. Napoleon launched a series of victories in the <a href="/wiki/Six_Days%27_Campaign" title="Six Days' Campaign">Six Days' Campaign</a>. While they repulsed the coalition forces and delayed the capture of Paris by at least a full month, these were not significant enough to turn the tide. The coalitionaries camped on the outskirts of the capital on 29 March. A day later, they advanced onto the demoralised soldiers protecting the city. Joseph Bonaparte led a final <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Paris_(1814)" title="Battle of Paris (1814)">battle</a> at the gates of Paris. They were greatly outnumbered, as 30,000 French soldiers were pitted against a combined coalition force that was 5 times greater than theirs. They were defeated, and Joseph retreated out of the city. The leaders of Paris surrendered to the Coalition on the last day of March 1814.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[200]</a></sup> On 1 April, Alexander addressed the <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A9nat_conservateur" title="Sénat conservateur">Sénat conservateur</a>. Long docile to Napoleon, under Talleyrand's prodding it had turned against him. Alexander told the Sénat that the Allies were fighting against Napoleon, not France, and they were prepared to offer honourable peace terms if Napoleon were removed from power. The next day, the Sénat passed the <a href="/wiki/Acte_de_d%C3%A9ch%C3%A9ance_de_l%27Empereur" title="Acte de déchéance de l'Empereur">Acte de déchéance de l'Empereur</a> ("Emperor's Demise Act"), which declared Napoleon deposed.
</p><p>Napoleon had advanced as far as <a href="/wiki/Fontainebleau" title="Fontainebleau">Fontainebleau</a> when he learned that Paris had fallen. When Napoleon proposed the army march on the capital, his senior officers and marshals mutinied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGates2003259_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGates2003259-209">[201]</a></sup> On 4 April, led by <a href="/wiki/Michel_Ney" title="Michel Ney">Ney</a>, the senior officers confronted Napoleon. When Napoleon asserted the army would follow him, Ney replied the army would follow its generals. While the ordinary soldiers and regimental officers wanted to fight on, the senior commanders were unwilling to continue. Without any senior officers or marshals, any prospective invasion of Paris would have been impossible. Bowing to the inevitable, on 4 April Napoleon abdicated in favour of his son, with Marie Louise as regent. However, the Allies refused to accept this under prodding from Alexander, who feared that Napoleon might find an excuse to retake the throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[202]</a></sup> Napoleon was then forced to announce his unconditional abdication only two days later.
</p><p>
In his farewell address to the soldiers of <a href="/wiki/Old_Guard_(France)" title="Old Guard (France)">Old Guard</a> in 20 April, Napoleon said:</p><blockquote><p>"Soldiers of my Old Guard, I have come to bid you farewell. For twenty years you have accompanied me faithfully on the paths of honor and glory. …With men like you, our cause was lost, but the war would have dragged on interminably, and it would have been a civil war. … So I am sacrificing our interests to those of our country. …Do not lament my fate; if I have agreed to live on, it is to serve our glory. I wish to write the history of the great deeds we have done together. Farewell, my children!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[203]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Exile_to_Elba">Exile to Elba</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beaume_-_Napol%C3%A9on_Ier_quittant_l%27%C3%AEle_d%27Elbe_-_1836.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1152" data-file-width="1947" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Beaume_-_Napol%C3%A9on_Ier_quittant_l%27%C3%AEle_d%27Elbe_-_1836.jpg/220px-Beaume_-_Napol%C3%A9on_Ier_quittant_l%27%C3%AEle_d%27Elbe_-_1836.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Beaume_-_Napol%C3%A9on_Ier_quittant_l%27%C3%AEle_d%27Elbe_-_1836.jpg/330px-Beaume_-_Napol%C3%A9on_Ier_quittant_l%27%C3%AEle_d%27Elbe_-_1836.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Beaume_-_Napol%C3%A9on_Ier_quittant_l%27%C3%AEle_d%27Elbe_-_1836.jpg/440px-Beaume_-_Napol%C3%A9on_Ier_quittant_l%27%C3%AEle_d%27Elbe_-_1836.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beaume_-_Napol%C3%A9on_Ier_quittant_l%27%C3%AEle_d%27Elbe_-_1836.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Napoleon leaving Elba</i> on 26 February 1815, by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Beaume" title="Joseph Beaume">Joseph Beaume</a> (1836)</div></div></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Allied Powers having declared that Emperor Napoleon was the sole obstacle to the restoration of peace in Europe, Emperor Napoleon, faithful to his oath, declares that he renounces, for himself and his heirs, the thrones of France and Italy, and that there is no personal sacrifice, even that of his life, which he is not ready to make in the interests of France.<br/>Done in the palace of Fontainebleau, 11 April 1814.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Act of abdication of Napoleon<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[204]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Fontainebleau_(1814)" title="Treaty of Fontainebleau (1814)">Treaty of Fontainebleau</a>, the Allies exiled Napoleon to <a href="/wiki/Elba" title="Elba">Elba</a>, an island of 12,000 inhabitants in the Mediterranean, 10 km (6 mi) off the <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">Tuscan</a> coast. They gave him <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Elba" title="Principality of Elba">sovereignty over the island</a> and allowed him to retain the title of <i>Emperor</i>. Napoleon attempted suicide with a pill he had carried after nearly being captured by the Russians during the retreat from Moscow. Its potency had weakened with age, however, and he survived to be exiled, while his wife and son took refuge in Austria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[205]</a></sup>
</p><p>He was conveyed to the island on <a href="/wiki/HMS_Undaunted_(1807)" title="HMS Undaunted (1807)">HMS <i>Undaunted</i></a> by Captain <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Ussher" title="Thomas Ussher">Thomas Ussher</a>, and he arrived at <a href="/wiki/Portoferraio" title="Portoferraio">Portoferraio</a> on 30 May 1814. In the first few months on Elba he created a small navy and army, developed the iron mines, oversaw the construction of new roads, issued decrees on modern agricultural methods, and overhauled the island's legal and educational system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[207]</a></sup>
</p><p>A few months into his exile, Napoleon learned that his ex-wife Josephine had died in France. He was devastated by the news, locking himself in his room and refusing to leave for two days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs.org_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-216">[208]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hundred_Days">Hundred Days</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days" title="Hundred Days">Hundred Days</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Retour_de_Napoleon_d%27_Isle_d%27Elbe,_by_Charles_de_Steuben.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1177" data-file-width="1574" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Retour_de_Napoleon_d%27_Isle_d%27Elbe%2C_by_Charles_de_Steuben.jpg/220px-Retour_de_Napoleon_d%27_Isle_d%27Elbe%2C_by_Charles_de_Steuben.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Retour_de_Napoleon_d%27_Isle_d%27Elbe%2C_by_Charles_de_Steuben.jpg/330px-Retour_de_Napoleon_d%27_Isle_d%27Elbe%2C_by_Charles_de_Steuben.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Retour_de_Napoleon_d%27_Isle_d%27Elbe%2C_by_Charles_de_Steuben.jpg/440px-Retour_de_Napoleon_d%27_Isle_d%27Elbe%2C_by_Charles_de_Steuben.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Retour_de_Napoleon_d%27_Isle_d%27Elbe,_by_Charles_de_Steuben.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Napoleon's Return from Elba</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Steuben" title="Charles de Steuben">Charles de Steuben</a>, 1818</div></div></div>
<p>Separated from his wife and son, who had returned to Austria, cut off from the allowance guaranteed to him by the Treaty of Fontainebleau, and aware of rumours he was about to be banished to a remote island in the Atlantic Ocean,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mclynn604_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mclynn604-217">[209]</a></sup> Napoleon escaped from Elba in the <a href="/wiki/Brig" title="Brig">brig</a> <a href="/wiki/French_brig_Inconstant_(1811)" title="French brig Inconstant (1811)"><i>Inconstant</i></a> on 26 February 1815 with 700 men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mclynn604_217-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mclynn604-217">[209]</a></sup> Two days later, he landed on the French mainland at <a href="/wiki/Golfe-Juan" title="Golfe-Juan">Golfe-Juan</a> and started heading north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mclynn604_217-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mclynn604-217">[209]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 5th Regiment was sent to intercept him and made contact <a href="/wiki/Route_Napol%C3%A9on" title="Route Napoléon">just south</a> of <a href="/wiki/Grenoble" title="Grenoble">Grenoble</a> on 7 March 1815. Napoleon approached the regiment alone, dismounted his horse and, when he was within gunshot range, shouted to the soldiers, "Here I am. Kill your Emperor, if you wish."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[210]</a></sup> The soldiers quickly responded with, "Vive L'Empereur!" Ney, who had boasted to the restored Bourbon king, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_XVIII_of_France" title="Louis XVIII of France">Louis XVIII</a>, that he would bring Napoleon to Paris in an iron cage, affectionately kissed his former emperor and forgot his oath of allegiance to the Bourbon monarch. The two then marched together toward Paris with a growing army. The unpopular Louis XVIII fled to Belgium after realizing that he had little political support. On 13 March, the powers at the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a> declared Napoleon an <a href="/wiki/Outlaw" title="Outlaw">outlaw</a>. Four days later, Great Britain, Russia, Austria, and Prussia each pledged to put 150,000 men into the field to end his rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[211]</a></sup>
</p><p>Napoleon arrived in Paris on 20 March and governed for a period now called the Hundred Days. By the start of June, the armed forces available to him had reached 200,000, and he decided to go on the offensive to attempt to drive a wedge between the oncoming British and Prussian armies. The <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_North_(France)" title="Army of the North (France)">French Army of the North</a> crossed the frontier into the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_the_Netherlands" title="United Kingdom of the Netherlands">United Kingdom of the Netherlands</a>, in modern-day Belgium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[212]</a></sup>
</p><p>Napoleon's forces fought two Coalition armies, commanded by the British <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">Duke of Wellington</a> and the Prussian <a href="/wiki/Gebhard_Leberecht_von_Bl%C3%BCcher" title="Gebhard Leberecht von Blücher">Prince Blücher</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Waterloo" title="Battle of Waterloo">Battle of Waterloo</a> on 18 June 1815. Wellington's army withstood repeated attacks by the French and drove them from the field while the Prussians arrived in force and broke through Napoleon's right flank.
</p><p>Napoleon returned to Paris and found that both the legislature and the people had turned against him. Realizing that his position was untenable, <a href="/wiki/Abdication_of_Napoleon,_1815" title="Abdication of Napoleon, 1815">he abdicated on 22 June in favour of his son</a>. He left Paris three days later and settled at Josephine's former palace in <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Malmaison" title="Château de Malmaison">Malmaison</a> (on the western bank of the <a href="/wiki/Seine" title="Seine">Seine</a> about 17 kilometres (11 mi) west of Paris). Even as Napoleon travelled to Paris, the Coalition forces swept through France (arriving in the vicinity of Paris on 29 June), with the stated intent of restoring Louis XVIII to the French throne.
</p><p>When Napoleon heard that Prussian troops had orders to capture him dead or alive, he fled to <a href="/wiki/Rochefort,_Charente-Maritime" title="Rochefort, Charente-Maritime">Rochefort</a>, considering an escape to the United States. British ships were blocking every port. Napoleon surrendered to <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Lewis_Maitland" title="Frederick Lewis Maitland">Captain Frederick Maitland</a> on <a href="/wiki/HMS_Bellerophon_(1786)" title="HMS Bellerophon (1786)">HMS <i>Bellerophon</i></a> on 15 July 1815.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[213]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exile_on_Saint_Helena">Exile on Saint Helena</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_sainthelene.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="563" data-file-width="758" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Napoleon_sainthelene.jpg/220px-Napoleon_sainthelene.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Napoleon_sainthelene.jpg/330px-Napoleon_sainthelene.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Napoleon_sainthelene.jpg/440px-Napoleon_sainthelene.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_sainthelene.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Napoleon on Saint Helena, watercolor by Franz Josef Sandmann, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1820</span></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Longwood_House_(16311222817).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Longwood_House_%2816311222817%29.jpg/220px-Longwood_House_%2816311222817%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Longwood_House_%2816311222817%29.jpg/330px-Longwood_House_%2816311222817%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Longwood_House_%2816311222817%29.jpg/440px-Longwood_House_%2816311222817%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Longwood_House_(16311222817).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Longwood_House" title="Longwood House">Longwood House</a>, Saint Helena, site of Napoleon's captivity</div></div></div>
<p>The British kept Napoleon on the island of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena" title="Saint Helena">Saint Helena</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>, 1,870 km (1,162 mi) from the west coast of Africa. They also took the precaution of sending a small garrison of soldiers to both <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena" title="Saint Helena">Saint Helena</a> and the uninhabited <a href="/wiki/Ascension_Island" title="Ascension Island">Ascension Island</a>, which lay between St. Helena and Europe, to prevent any escape from the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[214]</a></sup>
</p><p>Napoleon was moved to <a href="/wiki/Longwood_House" title="Longwood House">Longwood House</a> on Saint Helena in December 1815; it had fallen into disrepair, and the location was damp, windswept and unhealthy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[216]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> published articles insinuating the British government was trying to hasten his death. Napoleon often complained of the living conditions of Longwood House in letters to the island's governor and his custodian, <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Lowe" title="Hudson Lowe">Hudson Lowe</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[217]</a></sup> while his attendants complained of "colds, <a href="/wiki/Catarrh" title="Catarrh">catarrhs</a>, damp floors and poor provisions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[218]</a></sup> Modern scientists have speculated that his later illness may have arisen from <a href="/wiki/Arsenic_poisoning" title="Arsenic poisoning">arsenic poisoning</a> caused by <a href="/wiki/Scheele%27s_Green" title="Scheele's Green">copper arsenite</a> in the wallpaper at Longwood House.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p>With a small cadre of followers, Napoleon dictated his memoirs and grumbled about the living conditions. Lowe cut Napoleon's expenditure, ruled that no gifts were allowed if they mentioned his imperial status, and made his supporters sign a guarantee they would stay with the prisoner indefinitely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe-228">[220]</a></sup> When he held a dinner party, men were expected to wear military dress and "women [appeared] in evening gowns and gems. It was an explicit denial of the circumstances of his captivity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[221]</a></sup>
</p><p>While in exile, Napoleon wrote a book about <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a>, one of his great heroes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[222]</a></sup> He also studied <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> under the tutelage of <a href="/wiki/Emmanuel,_comte_de_Las_Cases" title="Emmanuel, comte de Las Cases">Count Emmanuel de Las Cases</a> with the main aim of being able to read English newspapers and books, as access to French newspapers and books was heavily restricted to him on Saint Helena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[223]</a></sup> Napoleon also devoted himself to compiling a book "<i>Mémorial de Ste-Hélène</i>", an account which reflected his self-depiction as a <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">liberal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enlightened_absolutism" title="Enlightened absolutism">visionary ruler</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_unification" title="European unification">European unification</a>, deposed by <a href="/wiki/Reactionary" title="Reactionary">reactionary</a> elements of the <i><a href="/wiki/Ancien_R%C3%A9gime" title="Ancien Régime">Ancien Régime</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto6_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto6-232">[224]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another pastime of Napoleon's while in exile was playing <a href="/wiki/Card_game" title="Card game">card games</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnold_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arnold-233">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[226]</a></sup> The number of <a href="/wiki/Patience_(game)" title="Patience (game)">patiences</a> named in his honour seems to suggest that he was an avid player of the solitary game. <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_at_St_Helena" title="Napoleon at St Helena">Napoleon at St Helena</a> is described as being a favourite of his,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[227]</a></sup> while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon%27s_Favourite" title="Napoleon's Favourite">Napoleon's Favourite</a> (or <a href="/wiki/St._Helena_(card_game)" title="St. Helena (card game)">St. Helena</a>) is clearly a contender. Other games with a Napoleonic theme include Napoleon's Flank, Napoleon's Shoulder, <a href="/wiki/Napoleon%27s_Square" title="Napoleon's Square">Napoleon's Square</a> and Little Napoleon Patience. However, Arnold argues that, while Napoleon played cards in exile, the notion that he played numerous patience games is "based on a misunderstanding".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnold_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-Arnold-233">[225]</a></sup>
</p><p>There were rumours of plots and even of his escape from Saint Helena, but in reality, no serious attempts were ever made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[228]</a></sup> For English poet <a href="/wiki/Lord_Byron" title="Lord Byron">Lord Byron</a>, Napoleon was the epitome of the <a href="/wiki/Romantic_hero" title="Romantic hero">Romantic hero</a>, the persecuted, lonely, and flawed genius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[229]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Death_mask_of_Napoleon" title="Death mask of Napoleon">Death mask of Napoleon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Retour_des_cendres" title="Retour des cendres">Retour des cendres</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Napoleon%27s_tomb" title="Napoleon's tomb">Napoleon's tomb</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_Skizze_von_Marryat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Napoleon_Skizze_von_Marryat.jpg/170px-Napoleon_Skizze_von_Marryat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Napoleon_Skizze_von_Marryat.jpg/255px-Napoleon_Skizze_von_Marryat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Napoleon_Skizze_von_Marryat.jpg/340px-Napoleon_Skizze_von_Marryat.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_Skizze_von_Marryat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Frederick_Marryat" title="Frederick Marryat">Frederick Marryat</a>'s sketch of Napoleon's body on his deathbed</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Death_mask_of_Napoleon-IMG_1535-black.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="5616" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Death_mask_of_Napoleon-IMG_1535-black.jpg/170px-Death_mask_of_Napoleon-IMG_1535-black.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Death_mask_of_Napoleon-IMG_1535-black.jpg/255px-Death_mask_of_Napoleon-IMG_1535-black.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Death_mask_of_Napoleon-IMG_1535-black.jpg/340px-Death_mask_of_Napoleon-IMG_1535-black.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Death_mask_of_Napoleon-IMG_1535-black.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Death mask of Napoleon</div></div></div>
<p>Napoleon's personal physician, <a href="/wiki/Barry_O%27Meara" title="Barry O'Meara">Barry O'Meara</a>, warned <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> that his declining state of health was mainly caused by the harsh treatment. During the last few years of his life, Napoleon confined himself for months on end in his damp, mold-infested and wretched habitation of Longwood. Years of isolation and <a href="/wiki/Loneliness" title="Loneliness">loneliness</a> started taking its toll and Napoleon began displaying serious symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Major_depressive_disorder" title="Major depressive disorder">depression</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto6_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto6-232">[224]</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 1821, Napoleon's health began to deteriorate rapidly, and he reconciled with the Catholic Church. By March, he had become confined to bed. Napoleon died on 5 May 1821 at Longwood House at age 51, after making his last confession, <a href="/wiki/Anointing_of_the_Sick_in_the_Catholic_Church" title="Anointing of the Sick in the Catholic Church">Extreme Unction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Viaticum" title="Viaticum">Viaticum</a> in the presence of Father Ange Vignali from his deathbed. His last words were, <i>France, l'armée, tête d'armée, Joséphine</i> ("France, the army, head of the army, Joséphine").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLynn655_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-McLynn655-239">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto6_232-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto6-232">[224]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shortly after his death, an <a href="/wiki/Autopsy" title="Autopsy">autopsy</a> was conducted and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francesco_Antommarchi" title="Francesco Antommarchi">Francesco Antommarchi</a>, the doctor conducting the autopsy, cut off <a href="/wiki/Napoleon%27s_penis" title="Napoleon's penis">Napoleon's penis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoberts2014_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts2014-33">[28]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:23_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-:23-241">[233]</a></sup> Napoleon's original <a href="/wiki/Death_mask" title="Death mask">death mask</a> was created around 6 May, although it is not clear which doctor created it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[i]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[235]</a></sup> Napoleon's heart and intestines were removed and contained separately in two sealed vessels, which were placed inside his coffin at his feet. In his will, he had asked to be buried on the banks of the <a href="/wiki/Seine" title="Seine">Seine</a>, but the British governor said he should be buried on Saint Helena, in the <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_the_Tomb" title="Valley of the Tomb">Valley of the Willows</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLynn655_239-1"><a href="#cite_note-McLynn655-239">[231]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Napoleone_Bonaparte%27s_Tomb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3580" data-file-width="4635" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Napoleone_Bonaparte%27s_Tomb.jpg/220px-Napoleone_Bonaparte%27s_Tomb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Napoleone_Bonaparte%27s_Tomb.jpg/330px-Napoleone_Bonaparte%27s_Tomb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Napoleone_Bonaparte%27s_Tomb.jpg/440px-Napoleone_Bonaparte%27s_Tomb.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Napoleone_Bonaparte%27s_Tomb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Napoleon%27s_tomb" title="Napoleon's tomb">Napoleon's tomb</a> at <a href="/wiki/Les_Invalides" title="Les Invalides">Les Invalides</a> in Paris</div></div></div>
<p>In 1840, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Philippe_I" title="Louis Philippe I">Louis Philippe I</a> obtained permission from the British government to return Napoleon's remains to France. His casket was opened to confirm that it still contained the former emperor. Despite being dead for nearly two decades, Napoleon had been very well preserved and not decomposed at all. On 15 December 1840, a <a href="/wiki/State_funeral" title="State funeral">state funeral</a> was held. The horse-drawn hearse proceeded from the <a href="/wiki/Arc_de_Triomphe" title="Arc de Triomphe">Arc de Triomphe</a> down the <a href="/wiki/Champs-%C3%89lys%C3%A9es" title="Champs-Élysées">Champs-Élysées</a>, across the <a href="/wiki/Place_de_la_Concorde" title="Place de la Concorde">Place de la Concorde</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Les_Invalides" title="Les Invalides">Esplanade des Invalides</a> and then to the cupola in St Jérôme's Chapel, where it remained until the <a href="/wiki/Napoleon%27s_tomb" title="Napoleon's tomb">tomb</a> designed by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Visconti" title="Louis Visconti">Louis Visconti</a> was completed.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:0160.0003_Lower_Parade,_James_Town,_St_Helena._With_a_view_of_the_remains_of_the_emperor_Napoleon_passing_down_on_the_13th_October_1840.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="723" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/0160.0003_Lower_Parade%2C_James_Town%2C_St_Helena._With_a_view_of_the_remains_of_the_emperor_Napoleon_passing_down_on_the_13th_October_1840.jpg/220px-0160.0003_Lower_Parade%2C_James_Town%2C_St_Helena._With_a_view_of_the_remains_of_the_emperor_Napoleon_passing_down_on_the_13th_October_1840.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/0160.0003_Lower_Parade%2C_James_Town%2C_St_Helena._With_a_view_of_the_remains_of_the_emperor_Napoleon_passing_down_on_the_13th_October_1840.jpg/330px-0160.0003_Lower_Parade%2C_James_Town%2C_St_Helena._With_a_view_of_the_remains_of_the_emperor_Napoleon_passing_down_on_the_13th_October_1840.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/0160.0003_Lower_Parade%2C_James_Town%2C_St_Helena._With_a_view_of_the_remains_of_the_emperor_Napoleon_passing_down_on_the_13th_October_1840.jpg/440px-0160.0003_Lower_Parade%2C_James_Town%2C_St_Helena._With_a_view_of_the_remains_of_the_emperor_Napoleon_passing_down_on_the_13th_October_1840.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:0160.0003_Lower_Parade,_James_Town,_St_Helena._With_a_view_of_the_remains_of_the_emperor_Napoleon_passing_down_on_the_13th_October_1840.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Napoleon's remains passing through <a href="/wiki/Jamestown,_Saint_Helena" title="Jamestown, Saint Helena">Jamestown, St Helena</a> on 13 October 1840</div></div></div>
<p>In 1861, Napoleon's remains were entombed in a <a href="/wiki/Sarcophagus" title="Sarcophagus">sarcophagus</a> of red <a href="/wiki/Quartzite" title="Quartzite">quartzite</a> from <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> (often mistaken for <a href="/wiki/Porphyry_(geology)" title="Porphyry (geology)">porphyry</a>) in the crypt under the dome at <a href="/wiki/Les_Invalides" title="Les Invalides">Les Invalides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[236]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cause_of_death">Cause of death</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_sur_son_lit_de_mort_Horace_Vernet_1826.jpg"><img alt="Gold-framed portrait painting of a gaunt middle-aged man with receding hair and laurel wreath, lying eyes-closed on white pillow with a white blanket covering to his neck and a gold Jesus cross resting on his chest" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1670" data-file-width="1829" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Napoleon_sur_son_lit_de_mort_Horace_Vernet_1826.jpg/220px-Napoleon_sur_son_lit_de_mort_Horace_Vernet_1826.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Napoleon_sur_son_lit_de_mort_Horace_Vernet_1826.jpg/330px-Napoleon_sur_son_lit_de_mort_Horace_Vernet_1826.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Napoleon_sur_son_lit_de_mort_Horace_Vernet_1826.jpg/440px-Napoleon_sur_son_lit_de_mort_Horace_Vernet_1826.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_sur_son_lit_de_mort_Horace_Vernet_1826.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Napoleon on His Death Bed</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Horace_Vernet" title="Horace Vernet">Horace Vernet</a>, 1826</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Napol%C3%A9on_%C3%A0_l%27ouverture_du_cercueil.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1054" data-file-width="1250" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Napol%C3%A9on_%C3%A0_l%27ouverture_du_cercueil.jpg/220px-Napol%C3%A9on_%C3%A0_l%27ouverture_du_cercueil.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Napol%C3%A9on_%C3%A0_l%27ouverture_du_cercueil.jpg/330px-Napol%C3%A9on_%C3%A0_l%27ouverture_du_cercueil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Napol%C3%A9on_%C3%A0_l%27ouverture_du_cercueil.jpg/440px-Napol%C3%A9on_%C3%A0_l%27ouverture_du_cercueil.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Napol%C3%A9on_%C3%A0_l%27ouverture_du_cercueil.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Situation of Napoleon's body when his coffin was reopened on St Helena, by Jules Rigo, 1840</div></div></div>
<p>The cause of Napoleon's death has been debated. His physician, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Carlo_Antommarchi" title="François Carlo Antommarchi">François Carlo Antommarchi</a>, led the autopsy, which found the cause of death to be <a href="/wiki/Stomach_cancer" title="Stomach cancer">stomach cancer</a>. Antommarchi did not sign the official report.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[237]</a></sup> Napoleon's father had died of stomach cancer, although this was apparently unknown at the time of the autopsy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[238]</a></sup> Antommarchi found evidence of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stomach_Ulcer" title="Stomach Ulcer">stomach ulcer</a>; this was the most convenient explanation for the British, who wanted to avoid criticism over their care of Napoleon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLynn655_239-2"><a href="#cite_note-McLynn655-239">[231]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1955, the diaries of Napoleon's valet, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Joseph_Marchand" title="Louis Joseph Marchand">Louis Marchand</a>, were published. His description of Napoleon in the months before his death led <a href="/wiki/Sten_Forshufvud" title="Sten Forshufvud">Sten Forshufvud</a> in a 1961 paper in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> to put forward other causes for his death, including deliberate <a href="/wiki/Arsenic_poisoning" title="Arsenic poisoning">arsenic poisoning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cullen_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cullen-248">[239]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">Arsenic</a> was used as a poison during the era because it was undetectable when administered over a long period. Furthermore, in a 1978 book with <a href="/wiki/Ben_Weider" title="Ben Weider">Ben Weider</a>, Forshufvud noted that Napoleon's body was found to be well preserved when moved in 1840. Arsenic is a strong preservative, and therefore this supported the poisoning hypothesis. Forshufvud and Weider observed that Napoleon had attempted to quench abnormal thirst by drinking large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Orgeat_syrup" title="Orgeat syrup">orgeat syrup</a> that contained cyanide compounds in the almonds used for flavouring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cullen_248-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cullen-248">[239]</a></sup> They maintained that the <a href="/wiki/Potassium_tartrate" title="Potassium tartrate">potassium tartrate</a> used in his treatment prevented his stomach from expelling these compounds and that his thirst was a symptom of the poison. Their hypothesis was that the <a href="/wiki/Calomel" title="Calomel">calomel</a> given to Napoleon became an overdose, which killed him and left extensive <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissue</a> damage behind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cullen_248-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cullen-248">[239]</a></sup> According to a 2007 article, the type of arsenic found in Napoleon's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hair_shaft" title="Hair shaft">hair shafts</a> was mineral, the most toxic, and according to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxicologist" title="Toxicologist">toxicologist</a> Patrick Kintz, this supported the conclusion that he was murdered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cullen156_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-cullen156-249">[240]</a></sup>
</p><p>There have been modern studies that have supported the original autopsy finding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cullen156_249-1"><a href="#cite_note-cullen156-249">[240]</a></sup> In a 2008 study, researchers analysed samples of Napoleon's hair from throughout his life, as well as samples from his family and other contemporaries. All samples had high levels of arsenic, approximately 100 times higher than the current average. According to these researchers, Napoleon's body was already heavily contaminated with arsenic as a boy, and the high arsenic concentration in his hair was not caused by intentional poisoning; people were constantly exposed to arsenic from glues and dyes throughout their lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[j]</a></sup> Studies published in 2007 and 2008 dismissed evidence of arsenic poisoning, suggesting <a href="/wiki/Peptic_ulcer_disease" title="Peptic ulcer disease">peptic ulcer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stomach_cancer" title="Stomach cancer">gastric cancer</a> as the cause of death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[242]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_and_the_Catholic_Church" title="Napoleon and the Catholic Church">Napoleon and the Catholic Church</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:France_dioceses_1801.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1412" data-file-width="1345" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/France_dioceses_1801.svg/220px-France_dioceses_1801.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/France_dioceses_1801.svg/330px-France_dioceses_1801.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/France_dioceses_1801.svg/440px-France_dioceses_1801.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:France_dioceses_1801.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reorganisation of the religious geography: France is divided into 59 <a href="/wiki/Diocese" title="Diocese">dioceses</a> and 10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_provinces" title="Ecclesiastical provinces">ecclesiastical provinces</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Napoleon was baptised in <a href="/wiki/Ajaccio" title="Ajaccio">Ajaccio</a> on 21 July 1771. He was raised as a Catholic but never developed much faith,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-napoleon.org_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-napoleon.org-253">[243]</a></sup> though he recalled the day of his <a href="/wiki/First_Communion" title="First Communion">First Communion</a> in the Catholic Church to be the happiest day of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wachtel1992_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wachtel1992-254">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joyce2005_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-Joyce2005-255">[245]</a></sup> As an adult, Napoleon was a <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deist</a>, believing in an absent and distant God. However, he had a keen appreciation of the power of organized religion in social and political affairs, and he paid a great deal of attention to bending it to his purposes. He noted the influence of Catholicism's rituals and splendors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-napoleon.org_253-1"><a href="#cite_note-napoleon.org-253">[243]</a></sup>
</p><p>Napoleon had a <a href="/wiki/Civil_marriage" title="Civil marriage">civil marriage</a> with Joséphine de Beauharnais, without religious ceremony. Napoleon was crowned Emperor on 2 December 1804 at <a href="/wiki/Notre-Dame_de_Paris" title="Notre-Dame de Paris">Notre-Dame de Paris</a> in a ceremony presided over by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_VII" title="Pope Pius VII">Pope Pius VII</a>. On the eve of the coronation ceremony, and at the insistence of Pope Pius VII, a private religious wedding ceremony of Napoleon and Joséphine was celebrated. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardinal_Fesch" title="Cardinal Fesch">Cardinal Fesch</a> performed the wedding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[246]</a></sup> This marriage was annulled by tribunals under Napoleon's control in January 1810. On 1 April 1810, Napoleon married the Austrian princess <a href="/wiki/Marie_Louise,_Duchess_of_Parma" title="Marie Louise, Duchess of Parma">Marie Louise</a> in a Catholic ceremony. Napoleon was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Excommunicated" title="Excommunicated">excommunicated</a> by the Pope through the bull <i><a href="/wiki/Quum_memoranda" title="Quum memoranda">Quum memoranda</a></i> in 1809, but later reconciled with the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> before his death in 1821.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[247]</a></sup> While in exile in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena" title="Saint Helena">Saint Helena</a> he is recorded to have said "I know men; and I tell you that <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus Christ</a> is not a man."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Responsibility_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-Responsibility-258">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[249]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[250]</a></sup>
He also defended <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> ("a great man") against Voltaire's <i><a href="/wiki/Mahomet_(play)" title="Mahomet (play)">Mahomet</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[251]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concordat">Concordat</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Concordat_of_1801" title="Concordat of 1801">Concordat of 1801</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FrenchChurchOathConcordat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="849" data-file-width="1076" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/FrenchChurchOathConcordat.jpg/220px-FrenchChurchOathConcordat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/FrenchChurchOathConcordat.jpg/330px-FrenchChurchOathConcordat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/FrenchChurchOathConcordat.jpg/440px-FrenchChurchOathConcordat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FrenchChurchOathConcordat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Leaders of the Catholic Church taking the civil oath required by <a href="/wiki/Concordat_of_1801" title="Concordat of 1801">the Concordat</a></div></div></div>
<p>Seeking national reconciliation between revolutionaries and Catholics, Napoleon and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_VII" title="Pope Pius VII">Pope Pius VII</a> signed the Concordat of 1801 on 15 July 1801. It solidified the Roman Catholic Church as the majority church of France and brought back most of its civil status. The hostility of devout Catholics against the state had now largely been resolved. The Concordat did not restore the vast church lands and endowments that had been seized during the revolution and sold off. As a part of the Concordat, Napoleon presented another set of laws called the <a href="/wiki/Organic_Articles" title="Organic Articles">Organic Articles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[253]</a></sup>
</p><p>While the Concordat restored much power to the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">papacy</a>, the balance of church–state relations had tilted firmly in Napoleon's favour. He selected the bishops and supervised church finances. Napoleon and the Pope both found the Concordat useful. Similar arrangements were made with the Church in territories controlled by Napoleon, especially Italy and Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[254]</a></sup> Now, Napoleon could win favour with the Catholics while also controlling Rome in a political sense. Napoleon said in April 1801, "Skillful conquerors have not got entangled with priests. They can both contain them and use them". French children were issued a catechism that taught them to love and respect Napoleon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[255]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrest_of_Pope_Pius_VII">Arrest of Pope Pius VII</span></h3>
<p>In 1809, under Napoleon's orders, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_VII" title="Pope Pius VII">Pope Pius VII</a> was placed under arrest in Italy, and in 1812 the prisoner Pontiff was transferred to France, being held in the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Fontainebleau" title="Palace of Fontainebleau">Palace of Fontainebleau</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-266">[256]</a></sup> Because the arrest was made in a clandestine manner, some sources<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[257]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_266-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-266">[256]</a></sup> describe it as a kidnapping. In January 1813, Napoleon personally forced the Pope to sign a humiliating "Concordat of Fontainebleau"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[258]</a></sup> which was later repudiated by the Pontiff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[259]</a></sup> The Pope was not released until 1814, when the Coalition invaded France.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_emancipation">Religious emancipation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_and_the_Jews" title="Napoleon and the Jews">Napoleon and the Jews</a> and <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_and_Protestants" title="Napoleon and Protestants">Napoleon and Protestants</a></div>
<p>Napoleon <a href="/wiki/Jewish_emancipation" title="Jewish emancipation">emancipated Jews</a>, as well as Protestants in Catholic countries and Catholics in Protestant countries, from laws which restricted them to <a href="/wiki/Ghetto" title="Ghetto">ghettos</a>, and he expanded their rights to property, worship, and careers. Despite the antisemitic reaction to Napoleon's policies from foreign governments and within France, he believed emancipation would benefit France by attracting Jews to the country given the restrictions they faced elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[260]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1806 an assembly of Jewish notables was gathered by Napoleon to discuss 12 questions broadly dealing with the relations between Jews and Christians, as well as other issues dealing with the Jewish ability to integrate into French society. Later, after the questions were answered in a satisfactory way according to the Emperor, a "<a href="/wiki/Grand_Sanhedrin" title="Grand Sanhedrin">great Sanhedrin</a>" was brought together to transform the answers into decisions that would form the basis of the future status of the Jews in France and the rest of the empire Napoleon was building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-271">[261]</a></sup>
</p><p>He stated, "I will never accept any proposals that will obligate the Jewish people to leave France, because to me the Jews are the same as any other citizen in our country. It takes weakness to chase them out of the country, but it takes strength to assimilate them".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[262]</a></sup> He was seen as so favourable to the Jews that the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a> formally condemned him as "<a href="/wiki/Antichrist" title="Antichrist">Antichrist</a> and the Enemy of God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[263]</a></sup>
</p><p>One year after the final meeting of the Sanhedrin, on 17 March 1808, Napoleon placed the Jews on probation. Several new laws restricting the citizenship the Jews had been offered 17 years previously were instituted at that time. However, despite pressure from leaders of a number of Christian communities to refrain from granting Jews emancipation, within one year of the issue of the new restrictions, they were once again lifted in response to the appeal of Jews from all over France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_271-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-271">[261]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Freemasonry">Freemasonry</span></h3>
<p>It is not known for certain if Napoleon was initiated into <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Freemasonry</a>. As Emperor, he appointed his brothers to Masonic offices under his jurisdiction: Louis was given the title of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Master_(Masonic)" title="Grand Master (Masonic)">Deputy Grand Master</a> in 1805; Jerome the title of Grand Master of the Grand Orient of Westphalia; Joseph was appointed Grand Master of the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Orient_de_France" title="Grand Orient de France">Grand Orient de France</a>; and finally Lucien was a member of the Grand Orient of France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[264]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality">Personality</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_visiting_the_Tribunat_(Palais_Royal)_in_1807.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="573" data-file-width="435" decoding="async" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Napoleon_visiting_the_Tribunat_%28Palais_Royal%29_in_1807.jpg/220px-Napoleon_visiting_the_Tribunat_%28Palais_Royal%29_in_1807.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Napoleon_visiting_the_Tribunat_%28Palais_Royal%29_in_1807.jpg/330px-Napoleon_visiting_the_Tribunat_%28Palais_Royal%29_in_1807.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4e/Napoleon_visiting_the_Tribunat_%28Palais_Royal%29_in_1807.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_visiting_the_Tribunat_(Palais_Royal)_in_1807.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Napoleon visiting the Palais Royal for the opening of the 8th session of the Tribunat in 1807, by <a href="/wiki/Merry-Joseph_Blondel" title="Merry-Joseph Blondel">Merry-Joseph Blondel</a></div></div></div>
<p>Historians emphasize the strength of the ambition that took Napoleon from an obscure village to rule over most of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[265]</a></sup> In-depth academic studies about <a href="#Education">his early life</a> conclude that up until age 2, he had a "gentle <a href="/wiki/Disposition" title="Disposition">disposition</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-34">[29]</a></sup> His older brother, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Bonaparte" title="Joseph Bonaparte">Joseph</a>, frequently received their mother's attention which made Napoleon more assertive and approval-driven. During his early schooling years, he would be harshly bullied by classmates for his Corsican identity and limited command of the <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French language</a>. To withstand the stress he became domineering, eventually developing an <a href="/wiki/Inferiority_complex" title="Inferiority complex">inferiority complex</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-34">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>George F. E. Rudé stresses his "rare combination of <a href="/wiki/Will_(philosophy)" title="Will (philosophy)">will</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intellect" title="Intellect">intellect</a> and physical <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/vigour" title="wikt:vigour">vigour</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[266]</a></sup> In one-on-one situations he typically had a hypnotic effect on people, seemingly bending the strongest leaders to his will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[267]</a></sup> He understood military technology, but was not an innovator in that regard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[268]</a></sup> He was an innovator in using the financial, bureaucratic, and diplomatic resources of France. He could rapidly dictate a series of complex commands to his subordinates, keeping in mind where major units were expected to be at each future point, and like a chess master, "seeing" the best plays moves ahead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-See_David_Chandler_1975_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-See_David_Chandler_1975-279">[269]</a></sup> This intellectual vigour was accompanied by a mixture of "remarkable charisma and willpower" and "a furious temper" exhibited during failure of his plans; which commanded respect as well as dread from his adjutants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[270]</a></sup>
</p><p>Napoleon maintained strict, efficient work habits, prioritizing what needed to be done. He cheated at cards, but repaid the losses; he had to win at everything he attempted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[271]</a></sup> He kept relays of staff and secretaries at work. Unlike many generals, Napoleon did not examine history to ask what <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">Hannibal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander</a> or anyone else did in a similar situation. Critics said he won many battles simply because of luck; Napoleon responded, "Give me lucky generals", arguing that "<a href="/wiki/Luck" title="Luck">luck</a>" comes to leaders who recognize opportunity, and seize it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[272]</a></sup> Dwyer states that Napoleon's victories at Austerlitz and Jena in 1805–06 heightened his sense of self-grandiosity, leaving him even more certain of his <a href="/wiki/Destiny" title="Destiny">destiny</a> and <a href="/wiki/Invincibility" title="Invincibility">invincibility</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[273]</a></sup> "I am of the race that founds empires" he once boasted, deeming himself an heir to the Ancient Romans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[274]</a></sup>
</p><p>In terms of influence on events, it was more than Napoleon's personality that took effect. He reorganized France itself to supply the men and money needed for wars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[275]</a></sup> He inspired his men—<a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">the Duke of Wellington</a> said his presence on the battlefield was worth 40,000 soldiers, for he inspired confidence from privates to field marshals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[276]</a></sup> The force of his personality neutralized material difficulties as his soldiers fought with the confidence that with Napoleon in charge they would surely win.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[277]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Image">Image</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_Napoleon" title="Cultural depictions of Napoleon">Cultural depictions of Napoleon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_in_1806.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1740" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Napoleon_in_1806.PNG/170px-Napoleon_in_1806.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Napoleon_in_1806.PNG/255px-Napoleon_in_1806.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Napoleon_in_1806.PNG/340px-Napoleon_in_1806.PNG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_in_1806.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Napoleon is often represented in his green colonel uniform of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chasseurs_%C3%A0_Cheval_de_la_Garde_Imp%C3%A9riale" title="Chasseurs à Cheval de la Garde Impériale">Chasseur à Cheval of the Imperial Guard</a>, the regiment that often served as his personal escort, with a large <a href="/wiki/Bicorne" title="Bicorne">bicorne</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Hand-in-waistcoat" title="Hand-in-waistcoat">hand-in-waistcoat</a> gesture.</div></div></div>
<p>The military historian <a href="/wiki/Martin_van_Creveld" title="Martin van Creveld">Martin van Creveld</a> has described him as "the most competent human being who ever lived".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[278]</a></sup> Since his death, many towns, streets, ships, and even cartoon characters have been named after him. He has been portrayed in hundreds of films and discussed in hundreds of thousands of books and articles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[279]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[280]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[281]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">German</a> legal scholar <a href="/wiki/Carl_Theodor_Welcker" title="Carl Theodor Welcker">Carl Theoder Welcker</a> described Napoleon as "the greatest master of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Machiavellism" title="Machiavellism">Machiavellism</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[282]</a></sup>
</p><p>When his contemporaries met him in person, many were surprised by his apparently unremarkable physical appearance in contrast to his significant deeds and reputation, especially in his youth, when he was consistently described as small and thin. <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Painter" title="Painter">painter</a> <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Farington" title="Joseph Farington">Joseph Farington</a>, who observed Napoleon personally in 1802, commented that "Samuel Rogers stood a little way from me and... seemed to be disappointed in the look of [Napoleon's] countenance ["face"] and said it was that of a little Italian." Farington said Napoleon's eyes were "lighter, and more of a grey, than I should have expected from his complexion", that "his person is below middle size", and that "his general aspect was milder than I had before thought it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[283]</a></sup>
</p><p>A personal friend of Napoleon's said that when he first met him in <a href="/wiki/Brienne-le-Ch%C3%A2teau" title="Brienne-le-Château">Brienne-le-Château</a> as a young man, Napoleon was only notable "for the dark color of his complexion, for his piercing and scrutinising glance, and for the style of his conversation"; he also said that Napoleon was personally a serious and somber man: "his conversation bore the appearance of ill-humor, and he was certainly not very amiable."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[284]</a></sup> Johann Ludwig Wurstemberger, who accompanied Napoleon from Camp Fornio in 1797 and on the Swiss campaign of 1798, noted that "Bonaparte was rather slight and emaciated-looking; his face, too, was very thin, with a dark complexion... his black, unpowdered hair hung down evenly over both shoulders", but that, despite his slight and unkempt appearance, "[h]is looks and expression were earnest and powerful."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[285]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Denis_Davydov" title="Denis Davydov">Denis Davydov</a> met him personally and considered him remarkably average in appearance:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>His face was slightly swarthy, with regular features. His nose was not very large, but straight, with a slight, hardly noticeable bend. The hair on his head was dark reddish-blond; his eyebrows and eyelashes were much darker than the colour of his hair, and his blue eyes, set off by the almost black lashes, gave him a most pleasing expression ... The man I saw was of short stature, just over five feet tall, rather heavy although he was only 37 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[286]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p><br/>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, he was taken seriously by the British press as a dangerous <a href="/wiki/Tyrant" title="Tyrant">tyrant</a>, poised to invade. Despite or due to his average size, Napoleon was mocked in British newspapers as a short tempered small man and he was nicknamed "Little Boney in a strong fit".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nationalpostBoney_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-nationalpostBoney-297">[287]</a></sup> A nursery rhyme warned children that Bonaparte ravenously ate naughty people; the "<a href="/wiki/Bogeyman" title="Bogeyman">bogeyman</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[288]</a></sup> The British propaganda about his supposedly small size was so successful that many people today "know" very little besides this untruth about him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[289]</a></sup> At 1.57 metres (5 ft 2 in), he had the height of an average French male but was short for an aristocrat or officer (partly why he was assigned to the artillery, since at the time the infantry and cavalry required more commanding figures).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Owen_Connelly_2006_7_300-0"><a href="#cite_note-Owen_Connelly_2006_7-300">[290]</a></sup> Some historians believe his size at death was incorrectly recorded due to use of an obsolete old French yardstick (a French foot equals 33 cm, while an English foot equals 30.47 cm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Owen_Connelly_2006_7_300-1"><a href="#cite_note-Owen_Connelly_2006_7-300">[290]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[291]</a></sup> But Napoleon was a champion of the metric system and had no use for the old yardsticks that had been out of use since 1793 in France. It is likely that he was 1.57 m (5 ft 2 in), the height measured on St. Helena (a British island), since he would have most likely been measured with an English yardstick rather than a yardstick of the French Old Regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Owen_Connelly_2006_7_300-2"><a href="#cite_note-Owen_Connelly_2006_7-300">[290]</a></sup> Napoleon surrounded himself with tall bodyguards and was affectionately nicknamed <i>le petit caporal</i> (the little corporal), reflecting his reported camaraderie with his soldiers rather than his height.
</p><p>When he became First Consul and later Emperor, Napoleon eschewed his general's uniform and habitually wore the green colonel uniform (non-Hussar) of a colonel of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chasseurs_%C3%A0_Cheval_de_la_Garde_Imp%C3%A9riale" title="Chasseurs à Cheval de la Garde Impériale">Chasseur à Cheval of the Imperial Guard</a>, the regiment that served as his personal escort many times, with a large <a href="/wiki/Bicorne" title="Bicorne">bicorne</a>. He also habitually wore (usually on Sundays) the blue uniform of a colonel of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Guard_(Napoleon_I)" title="Imperial Guard (Napoleon I)">Imperial Guard</a> Foot Grenadiers (blue with white facings and red cuffs). He also wore his <a href="/wiki/Legion_of_Honour" title="Legion of Honour">Légion d'honneur</a> star, medal and ribbon, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Iron_Crown" title="Order of the Iron Crown">Order of the Iron Crown</a> decorations, white French-style <a href="/wiki/Culottes" title="Culottes">culottes</a> and white stockings. This was in contrast to the complex uniforms with many decorations of his <a href="/wiki/Marshal_of_the_Empire" title="Marshal of the Empire">marshals</a> and those around him.
</p><p>In his later years he gained quite a bit of weight and had a complexion considered pale or sallow, something contemporaries took note of. Novelist Paul de Kock, who saw him in 1811 on the balcony of the Tuileries, called Napoleon "yellow, obese, and bloated".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[292]</a></sup> A British captain who met him in 1815 stated "I felt very much disappointed, as I believe everyone else did, in his appearance ... He is fat, rather what we call pot-bellied, and although his leg is well shaped, it is rather clumsy ... He is very sallow, with light grey eyes, and rather thin, greasy-looking brown hair, and altogether a very nasty, priestlike-looking fellow."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[293]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Stock_character" title="Stock character">stock character</a> of Napoleon is a comically short "petty tyrant" and this has become a cliché in popular culture. He is often portrayed wearing a large <a href="/wiki/Bicorne" title="Bicorne">bicorne</a> hat—sideways—with a <a href="/wiki/Hand-in-waistcoat" title="Hand-in-waistcoat">hand-in-waistcoat</a> gesture—a reference to the painting produced in 1812 by <a href="/wiki/Jacques-Louis_David" title="Jacques-Louis David">Jacques-Louis David</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBordes2007118_304-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBordes2007118-304">[294]</a></sup> In 1908 <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Adler" title="Alfred Adler">Alfred Adler</a>, a psychologist, cited Napoleon to describe an <a href="/wiki/Inferiority_complex" title="Inferiority complex">inferiority complex</a> in which short people adopt an over-aggressive behaviour to compensate for lack of height; this inspired the term <i><a href="/wiki/Napoleon_complex" title="Napoleon complex">Napoleon complex</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[295]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reforms">Reforms</span></h2>
