<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Debret_-_Premiere_distribution_des_decorations_de_la_Legion_d%27honneur.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2272" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Debret_-_Premiere_distribution_des_decorations_de_la_Legion_d%27honneur.jpg/220px-Debret_-_Premiere_distribution_des_decorations_de_la_Legion_d%27honneur.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Debret_-_Premiere_distribution_des_decorations_de_la_Legion_d%27honneur.jpg/330px-Debret_-_Premiere_distribution_des_decorations_de_la_Legion_d%27honneur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Debret_-_Premiere_distribution_des_decorations_de_la_Legion_d%27honneur.jpg/440px-Debret_-_Premiere_distribution_des_decorations_de_la_Legion_d%27honneur.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Debret_-_Premiere_distribution_des_decorations_de_la_Legion_d%27honneur.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First remittance of the Légion d'Honneur, 15 July 1804, at <a href="/wiki/Les_Invalides" title="Les Invalides">Saint-Louis des Invalides</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Debret" title="Jean-Baptiste Debret">Jean-Baptiste Debret</a> (1812)</div></div></div>
<p>Napoleon instituted various reforms, such as <a href="/wiki/Higher_education" title="Higher education">higher education</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Tax_law" title="Tax law">tax code</a>, road and sewer systems, and established the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_France" title="Bank of France">Banque de France</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/Central_bank" title="Central bank">central bank</a> in French history. He negotiated the <a href="/wiki/Concordat_of_1801" title="Concordat of 1801">Concordat of 1801</a> with the Catholic Church, which sought to reconcile the mostly Catholic population to his regime. It was presented alongside the <a href="/wiki/Organic_Articles" title="Organic Articles">Organic Articles</a>, which regulated public worship in France. He dissolved the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> prior to <a href="/wiki/Unification_of_Germany" title="Unification of Germany">German Unification</a> later in the 19th century. The sale of the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Territory" title="Louisiana Territory">Louisiana Territory</a> to the United States doubled the size of the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGraw-Hill_306-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGraw-Hill-306">[296]</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 1802, he instituted the <a href="/wiki/Legion_of_Honour" title="Legion of Honour">Legion of Honour</a>, a substitute for the old royalist decorations and <a href="/wiki/Order_of_chivalry" title="Order of chivalry">orders of chivalry</a>, to encourage civilian and military achievements; the order is still the highest decoration in France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[297]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Napoleonic_Code">Napoleonic Code</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Code" title="Napoleonic Code">Napoleonic Code</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Code_Civil_1804.png"><img alt="Page of French writing" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="762" data-file-width="636" decoding="async" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Code_Civil_1804.png/220px-Code_Civil_1804.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Code_Civil_1804.png/330px-Code_Civil_1804.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Code_Civil_1804.png/440px-Code_Civil_1804.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Code_Civil_1804.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First page of the 1804 original edition of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_code" title="Civil code">Code Civil</a></div></div></div>
<p>Napoleon's <a href="/wiki/Civil_code" title="Civil code">set of civil laws</a>, the <i>Code Civil</i>—now often known as the Napoleonic Code—was prepared by committees of legal experts under the supervision of <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques-R%C3%A9gis_de_Cambac%C3%A9r%C3%A8s" title="Jean-Jacques-Régis de Cambacérès">Jean Jacques Régis de Cambacérès</a>, the <i>Second Consul</i>. Napoleon participated actively in the sessions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_of_State_(France)" title="Council of State (France)">Council of State</a> that revised the drafts. The development of the code was a fundamental change in the nature of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> legal system with its stress on clearly written and accessible law. Other codes ("<a href="/wiki/Les_cinq_codes" title="Les cinq codes">Les cinq codes</a>") were commissioned by Napoleon to codify criminal and commerce law; a Code of Criminal Instruction was published, which enacted rules of <a href="/wiki/Due_process" title="Due process">due process</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[298]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Napoleonic code was adopted throughout much of Continental Europe, though only in the lands he conquered, and remained in force after Napoleon's defeat. Napoleon said: "My true glory is not to have won forty battles ... Waterloo will erase the memory of so many victories. ... But ... what will live forever, is my Civil Code".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[299]</a></sup> The Code influences a quarter of the world's jurisdictions such as those in Continental Europe, the Americas, and Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[300]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dieter Langewiesche described the code as a "revolutionary project" that spurred the development of <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">bourgeois society</a> in Germany by the extension of the right to own property and an acceleration towards the end of <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a>. Napoleon reorganized what had been the Holy Roman Empire, made up of about three hundred <i><a href="/wiki/Kleinstaaterei" title="Kleinstaaterei">Kleinstaaterei</a></i>, into a more streamlined forty-state <a href="/wiki/Confederation_of_the_Rhine" title="Confederation of the Rhine">Confederation of the Rhine</a>; this helped promote the <a href="/wiki/German_Confederation" title="German Confederation">German Confederation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Unification_of_Germany" title="Unification of Germany">unification of Germany</a> in 1871.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[301]</a></sup>
</p><p>The movement toward <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_unification" title="Italian unification">Italian unification</a> was similarly precipitated by Napoleonic rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[302]</a></sup> These changes contributed to the development of nationalism and the <a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">nation state</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[303]</a></sup>
</p><p>Napoleon implemented a wide array of liberal reforms in France and across Continental Europe, especially in Italy and Germany, as summarized by British historian <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Roberts_(historian)" title="Andrew Roberts (historian)">Andrew Roberts</a>:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The ideas that underpin our modern world—meritocracy, equality before the law, property rights, religious toleration, modern secular education, sound finances, and so on—were championed, consolidated, codified and geographically extended by Napoleon. To them he added a rational and efficient local administration, an end to rural banditry, the encouragement of science and the arts, the abolition of feudalism and the greatest codification of laws since the fall of the Roman Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[304]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Napoleon directly overthrew remnants of feudalism in much of western Continental Europe. He liberalized <a href="/wiki/Property_law" title="Property law">property laws</a>, ended <a href="/wiki/Manorialism" title="Manorialism">seigneurial dues</a>, abolished the <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guild</a> of merchants and craftsmen to facilitate entrepreneurship, legalized divorce, closed the <a href="/wiki/Ghetto" title="Ghetto">Jewish ghettos</a> and made Jews equal to everyone else. The <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Inquisition" title="Spanish Inquisition">Inquisition</a> ended as did the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>. The power of church courts and religious authority was sharply reduced and <a href="/wiki/Equality_before_the_law" title="Equality before the law">equality under the law</a> was proclaimed for all men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[305]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare">Warfare</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_weaponry_and_warfare" title="Napoleonic weaponry and warfare">Napoleonic weaponry and warfare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Military_career_of_Napoleon_Bonaparte" title="Military career of Napoleon Bonaparte">Military career of Napoleon Bonaparte</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_a_Cherbourg_bordercropped.jpg"><img alt="Photo of a grey and phosphorous-coloured equestrian statue. Napoleon is seated on the horse, which is rearing up, he looks forward with his right hand raised and pointing forward; his left hand holds the reins." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="794" data-file-width="594" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Napoleon_a_Cherbourg_bordercropped.jpg/170px-Napoleon_a_Cherbourg_bordercropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Napoleon_a_Cherbourg_bordercropped.jpg/255px-Napoleon_a_Cherbourg_bordercropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Napoleon_a_Cherbourg_bordercropped.jpg/340px-Napoleon_a_Cherbourg_bordercropped.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_a_Cherbourg_bordercropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue in <a href="/wiki/Cherbourg-Octeville" title="Cherbourg-Octeville">Cherbourg-Octeville</a> unveiled by Napoleon III in 1858. Napoleon I strengthened the town's defences to prevent British naval incursions.</div></div></div>
<p>In the field of <a href="/wiki/Military_organization" title="Military organization">military organization</a>, Napoleon borrowed from previous theorists such as <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Antoine_Hippolyte,_Comte_de_Guibert" title="Jacques Antoine Hippolyte, Comte de Guibert">Jacques Antoine Hippolyte, Comte de Guibert</a>, and from the reforms of preceding French governments, and then developed much of what was already in place. He continued the policy, which emerged from the Revolution, of promotion based primarily on merit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Archer_316-0"><a href="#cite_note-Archer-316">[306]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Corps" title="Corps">Corps</a> replaced divisions as the largest army units, <a href="/wiki/Self-propelled_artillery" title="Self-propelled artillery">mobile artillery</a> was integrated into reserve batteries, the staff system became more fluid and cavalry returned as an important formation in French military doctrine. These methods are now referred to as essential features of Napoleonic warfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Archer_316-1"><a href="#cite_note-Archer-316">[306]</a></sup> Though he consolidated the practice of modern <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscription</a> introduced by the Directory, one of the restored monarchy's first acts was to end it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[307]</a></sup>
</p><p>His opponents learned from Napoleon's innovations. The increased importance of artillery after 1807 stemmed from his creation of a highly mobile artillery force, the growth in artillery numbers, and changes in artillery practices. As a result of these factors, Napoleon, rather than relying on infantry to wear away the enemy's defences, now could use massed artillery as a spearhead to pound a break in the enemy's line that was then exploited by supporting infantry and cavalry. McConachy rejects the alternative theory that growing reliance on artillery by the French army beginning in 1807 was an outgrowth of the declining quality of the French infantry and, later, France's inferiority in cavalry numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[308]</a></sup> Weapons and other kinds of military technology remained static through the Revolutionary and Napoleonic eras, but 18th-century <a href="/wiki/Operational_level_of_war" title="Operational level of war">operational mobility</a> underwent change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[309]</a></sup>
</p><p>Napoleon's biggest influence was in the conduct of warfare. <a href="/wiki/Antoine-Henri_Jomini" title="Antoine-Henri Jomini">Antoine-Henri Jomini</a> explained Napoleon's methods in a widely used textbook that influenced all European and American armies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[310]</a></sup> Napoleon was regarded by the influential military theorist <a href="/wiki/Carl_von_Clausewitz" title="Carl von Clausewitz">Carl von Clausewitz</a> as a genius in the operational art of war, and historians rank him as a great military commander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321">[311]</a></sup> Wellington, when asked who was the greatest general of the day, answered: "In this age, in past ages, in any age, Napoleon".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[312]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:CITESHORT" title="Wikipedia:CITESHORT"><span title="More information is required to link this short citation to its long citation. (August 2021)">incomplete short citation</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Under Napoleon, a new emphasis towards the destruction, not just outmaneuvering, of enemy armies emerged. Invasions of enemy territory occurred over broader fronts which made wars costlier and more decisive. The political effect of war increased; defeat for a European power meant more than the loss of isolated enclaves. Near-<a href="/wiki/Carthaginian_peace" title="Carthaginian peace">Carthaginian peaces</a> intertwined whole national efforts, intensifying the Revolutionary phenomenon of total war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[313]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metric_system">Metric system</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_metric_system" title="History of the metric system">History of the metric system</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesures_usuelles" title="Mesures usuelles">Mesures usuelles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Units_of_measurement_in_France" title="Units of measurement in France">Units of measurement in France</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 202px;">
<div class="thumbimage" style="width: 200px; height: 200px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -5px; left: -6px; width: 410px"><div class="noresize"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:France_1803-04-A_20_Francs.jpg"><img alt="Depicted as First Consul on the 1803 20 gold Napoléon gold coin" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="4827" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/France_1803-04-A_20_Francs.jpg/410px-France_1803-04-A_20_Francs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/France_1803-04-A_20_Francs.jpg/615px-France_1803-04-A_20_Francs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/France_1803-04-A_20_Francs.jpg/820px-France_1803-04-A_20_Francs.jpg 2x" width="410"/></a></div></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:France_1803-04-A_20_Francs.jpg" title="File:France 1803-04-A 20 Francs.jpg"> </a></div>Depicted as <a href="/wiki/French_Consulate" title="French Consulate">First Consul</a> on the 1803 20 gold <a href="/wiki/Napol%C3%A9on_(coin)" title="Napoléon (coin)">Napoléon</a> gold coin
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The official introduction of the metric system in September 1799 was unpopular in large sections of French society. Napoleon's rule greatly aided adoption of the new standard not only across France but also across the French <a href="/wiki/Sphere_of_influence" title="Sphere of influence">sphere of influence</a>. Napoleon took a retrograde step in 1812 when he passed legislation to introduce the <i><a href="/wiki/Mesures_usuelles" title="Mesures usuelles">mesures usuelles</a></i> (traditional units of measurement) for retail trade,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H&amp;H_324-0"><a href="#cite_note-H&amp;H-324">[314]</a></sup> a system of measure that resembled the pre-revolutionary units but were based on the kilogram and the metre; for example, the <i>livre metrique</i> (metric pound) was 500 g,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historique_325-0"><a href="#cite_note-historique-325">[315]</a></sup> in contrast to the value of the <i>livre du roi</i> (the king's pound), 489.5 g.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[316]</a></sup> Other units of measure were rounded in a similar manner prior to the definitive introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Metric_system" title="Metric system">metric system</a> across parts of Europe in the middle of the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[317]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3>
<p>Napoleon's educational reforms laid the foundation of a modern system of education in France and throughout much of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[318]</a></sup> Napoleon synthesized the best academic elements from the <i><a href="/wiki/Ancien_R%C3%A9gime" title="Ancien Régime">Ancien Régime</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">The Enlightenment</a>, and <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">the Revolution</a>, with the aim of establishing a stable, well-educated and prosperous society. He made French the only official language. He left some primary education in the hands of religious orders, but he offered public support to secondary education. Napoleon founded a number of state secondary schools (<i><a href="/wiki/Secondary_education_in_France#Lycée" title="Secondary education in France">lycées</a></i>) designed to produce a standardized education that was uniform across France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto5_329-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto5-329">[319]</a></sup>
</p><p>All students were taught the sciences along with modern and classical languages. Unlike the system during the <i>Ancien Régime</i>, religious topics did not dominate the curriculum, although they were present with the teachers from the clergy. Napoleon hoped to use religion to produce social stability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto5_329-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto5-329">[319]</a></sup> He gave special attention to the advanced centers, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%89cole_Polytechnique" title="École Polytechnique">École Polytechnique</a>, that provided both military expertise and state-of-the-art research in science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[320]</a></sup> Napoleon made some of the first efforts at establishing a system of secular and public education.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2016)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> The system featured scholarships and strict discipline, with the result being a French educational system that outperformed its European counterparts, many of which borrowed from the French system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[321]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Memory_and_evaluation">Memory and evaluation</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Legacy_and_memory_of_Napoleon" title="Legacy and memory of Napoleon">Legacy and memory of Napoleon</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:El_Tres_de_Mayo,_by_Francisco_de_Goya,_from_Prado_thin_black_margin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="22626" data-file-width="29294" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/El_Tres_de_Mayo%2C_by_Francisco_de_Goya%2C_from_Prado_thin_black_margin.jpg/220px-El_Tres_de_Mayo%2C_by_Francisco_de_Goya%2C_from_Prado_thin_black_margin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/El_Tres_de_Mayo%2C_by_Francisco_de_Goya%2C_from_Prado_thin_black_margin.jpg/330px-El_Tres_de_Mayo%2C_by_Francisco_de_Goya%2C_from_Prado_thin_black_margin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/El_Tres_de_Mayo%2C_by_Francisco_de_Goya%2C_from_Prado_thin_black_margin.jpg/440px-El_Tres_de_Mayo%2C_by_Francisco_de_Goya%2C_from_Prado_thin_black_margin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:El_Tres_de_Mayo,_by_Francisco_de_Goya,_from_Prado_thin_black_margin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Third_of_May_1808" title="The Third of May 1808">The Third of May 1808</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Goya" title="Francisco Goya">Francisco Goya</a>, showing Spanish resisters being executed by French troops</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plate_L_from_%27An_Historical_Account_of_the_Campaign_in_the_Netherlands%27_by_William_Mudford_(1817).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1236" data-file-width="1629" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Plate_L_from_%27An_Historical_Account_of_the_Campaign_in_the_Netherlands%27_by_William_Mudford_%281817%29.jpg/220px-Plate_L_from_%27An_Historical_Account_of_the_Campaign_in_the_Netherlands%27_by_William_Mudford_%281817%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Plate_L_from_%27An_Historical_Account_of_the_Campaign_in_the_Netherlands%27_by_William_Mudford_%281817%29.jpg/330px-Plate_L_from_%27An_Historical_Account_of_the_Campaign_in_the_Netherlands%27_by_William_Mudford_%281817%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Plate_L_from_%27An_Historical_Account_of_the_Campaign_in_the_Netherlands%27_by_William_Mudford_%281817%29.jpg/440px-Plate_L_from_%27An_Historical_Account_of_the_Campaign_in_the_Netherlands%27_by_William_Mudford_%281817%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plate_L_from_%27An_Historical_Account_of_the_Campaign_in_the_Netherlands%27_by_William_Mudford_(1817).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A mass grave of soldiers killed at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Waterloo" title="Battle of Waterloo">Battle of Waterloo</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the political realm, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historians" title="Historians">historians</a> debate whether Napoleon was "an <a href="/wiki/Enlightened_absolutism" title="Enlightened absolutism">enlightened despot</a> who laid the foundations of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_Europe" title="Modern Europe">modern Europe</a>" or "a <a href="/wiki/Narcissistic_personality_disorder" title="Narcissistic personality disorder">megalomaniac</a> who wrought greater misery than any man before the coming of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hitler" title="Hitler">Hitler</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[322]</a></sup> Many historians have concluded that he had grandiose foreign policy ambitions. The Continental powers as late as 1808 were willing to give him nearly all of his gains and titles, but some scholars maintain he was overly aggressive and pushed for too much, until his empire collapsed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[323]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[324]</a></sup>
</p><p>He was considered a <a href="/wiki/Tyrant" title="Tyrant">tyrant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Usurper" title="Usurper">usurper</a> by his opponents at the time and ever since. His critics charge that he was not troubled when faced with the prospect of war and death for thousands, turned his search for undisputed rule into a series of conflicts throughout Europe and ignored treaties and conventions alike.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M666_335-0"><a href="#cite_note-M666-335">[325]</a></sup> His role in the <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Revolution" title="Haitian Revolution">Haitian Revolution</a> and decision to reinstate slavery in France's overseas colonies are controversial and affect his reputation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[326]</a></sup> French <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">liberal</a> intellectual <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Constant" title="Benjamin Constant">Benjamin Constant</a> (1767–1830) was a staunch critic of political homogenisation and <a href="/wiki/Cult_of_personality" title="Cult of personality">personality cult</a> that dominated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleonic_France" title="Napoleonic France">Napoleonic France</a> and wrote several books condemning Napoleon such as "<i><a class="external text" href="https://fr.m.wikisource.org/wiki/De_l%E2%80%99esprit_de_conqu%C3%AAte_et_de_l%E2%80%99usurpation_dans_leur_rapports_avec_la_civilisation_europ%C3%A9enne">The Spirit of Conquest and Usurpation</a></i>" (1814) and "<i>Principles of Politics Applicable to All Representative Governments</i>" (1815). According to Constant, <a href="/wiki/Bonapartism" title="Bonapartism">Bonapartism</a> was even more tyrannical than the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bourbon_monarchy" title="Bourbon monarchy">Bourbon monarchy</a>, since it forced the masses to support its grand universalist narrative through <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jingoism" title="Jingoism">jingoism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[327]</a></sup>
</p><p>Napoleon institutionalized plunder of conquered territories: French museums contain art stolen by Napoleon's forces from across Europe. Artefacts were brought to the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Musée du Louvre</a> for a grand central museum; an example which would later be followed by others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[328]</a></sup> He was compared to <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> by the historian <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Geyl" title="Pieter Geyl">Pieter Geyl</a> in 1947,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[329]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Claude_Ribbe" title="Claude Ribbe">Claude Ribbe</a> in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[330]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_G._Chandler" title="David G. Chandler">David G. Chandler</a>, a historian of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleonic_warfare" title="Napoleonic warfare">Napoleonic warfare</a>, wrote in 1973 that, "Nothing could be more degrading to the former [Napoleon] and more flattering to the latter [Hitler]. The comparison is odious. On the whole Napoleon was inspired by a noble dream, wholly dissimilar from Hitler's... Napoleon left great and lasting testimonies to his genius—in codes of law and national identities which survive to the present day. Adolf Hitler left nothing but destruction."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChandler1973xliii_341-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChandler1973xliii-341">[331]</a></sup>
</p><p>Critics argue Napoleon's true legacy must reflect the loss of status for France and needless deaths brought by his rule: historian <a href="/wiki/Victor_Davis_Hanson" title="Victor Davis Hanson">Victor Davis Hanson</a> writes, "After all, the military record is unquestioned—17 years of wars, perhaps <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars_casualties" title="Napoleonic Wars casualties">six million Europeans dead</a>, France bankrupt, her overseas colonies lost."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[332]</a></sup> McLynn states that, "He can be viewed as the man who set back European economic life for a generation by the dislocating impact of his wars."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M666_335-1"><a href="#cite_note-M666-335">[325]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vincent_Cronin" title="Vincent Cronin">Vincent Cronin</a> replies that such criticism relies on the flawed premise that Napoleon was responsible for the wars which bear his name, when in fact France was the victim of a series of coalitions that aimed to destroy the ideals of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-c342_343-0"><a href="#cite_note-c342-343">[333]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> military historian <a href="/wiki/Correlli_Barnett" title="Correlli Barnett">Correlli Barnett</a> calls him "a social misfit" who exploited France for his personal megalomaniac goals. He says Napoleon's reputation is exaggerated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-344"><a href="#cite_note-344">[334]</a></sup> French scholar <a href="/wiki/Jean_Tulard" title="Jean Tulard">Jean Tulard</a> provided an influential account of his image as a saviour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[335]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Louis_Bergeron" title="Louis Bergeron">Louis Bergeron</a> has praised the numerous changes he made to French society, especially regarding the law as well as education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-346"><a href="#cite_note-346">[336]</a></sup> His greatest failure was the <a href="/wiki/French_invasion_of_Russia" title="French invasion of Russia">Russian invasion</a>. Many historians have blamed Napoleon's poor planning, but Russian scholars instead emphasize the Russian response, noting the notorious winter weather was just as hard on the defenders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347">[337]</a></sup>
</p><p>The large and growing historiography in French, English, Russian, Spanish and other languages has been summarized and evaluated by numerous scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348">[338]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-349"><a href="#cite_note-349">[339]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350"><a href="#cite_note-350">[340]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Propaganda_and_memory">Propaganda and memory</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Napoleon%27s_exile_to_Elba3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2910" data-file-width="3545" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Napoleon%27s_exile_to_Elba3.jpg/220px-Napoleon%27s_exile_to_Elba3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Napoleon%27s_exile_to_Elba3.jpg/330px-Napoleon%27s_exile_to_Elba3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Napoleon%27s_exile_to_Elba3.jpg/440px-Napoleon%27s_exile_to_Elba3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Napoleon%27s_exile_to_Elba3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1814 caricature of Napoleon being exiled to Elba: the ex-emperor is riding a donkey backwards while holding a broken sword.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_propaganda" title="Napoleonic propaganda">Napoleonic propaganda</a></div>
<p>Napoleon's use of <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a> contributed to his rise to power, legitimated his régime, and established his image for posterity. Strict <a href="/wiki/Censorship" title="Censorship">censorship</a>, controlling various key constituents of the <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">press</a>, books, <a href="/wiki/Theatre" title="Theatre">theatre</a>, and art were part of his propaganda scheme, aimed at portraying him as bringing desperately wanted peace and stability to France. The propagandistic <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetoric</a> changed in relation to events and to the atmosphere of Napoleon's reign, focusing first on his role as a general in the army and identification as a soldier, and moving to his role as emperor and a civil leader. Specifically targeting his civilian audience, Napoleon fostered a relationship with the contemporary art community, taking an active role in commissioning and controlling different forms of art production to suit his propaganda goals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-351"><a href="#cite_note-351">[341]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pichet_Bonaparte_d%C3%A9tr%C3%B4n%C3%A9_-_Mus%C3%A9e_de_la_R%C3%A9volution_fran%C3%A7aise.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3392" data-file-width="2094" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Pichet_Bonaparte_d%C3%A9tr%C3%B4n%C3%A9_-_Mus%C3%A9e_de_la_R%C3%A9volution_fran%C3%A7aise.jpg/170px-Pichet_Bonaparte_d%C3%A9tr%C3%B4n%C3%A9_-_Mus%C3%A9e_de_la_R%C3%A9volution_fran%C3%A7aise.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Pichet_Bonaparte_d%C3%A9tr%C3%B4n%C3%A9_-_Mus%C3%A9e_de_la_R%C3%A9volution_fran%C3%A7aise.jpg/255px-Pichet_Bonaparte_d%C3%A9tr%C3%B4n%C3%A9_-_Mus%C3%A9e_de_la_R%C3%A9volution_fran%C3%A7aise.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Pichet_Bonaparte_d%C3%A9tr%C3%B4n%C3%A9_-_Mus%C3%A9e_de_la_R%C3%A9volution_fran%C3%A7aise.jpg/340px-Pichet_Bonaparte_d%C3%A9tr%C3%B4n%C3%A9_-_Mus%C3%A9e_de_la_R%C3%A9volution_fran%C3%A7aise.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pichet_Bonaparte_d%C3%A9tr%C3%B4n%C3%A9_-_Mus%C3%A9e_de_la_R%C3%A9volution_fran%C3%A7aise.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ceramic pitcher of Bonaparte: <i>Where is he going to. To Elba.</i> (<a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_de_la_R%C3%A9volution_fran%C3%A7aise" title="Musée de la Révolution française">Musée de la Révolution française</a>).</div></div></div>
<p>In England, Russia and across Europe—though not in France—Napoleon was a popular topic of <a href="/wiki/Caricature" title="Caricature">caricature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-352"><a href="#cite_note-352">[342]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[343]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-354"><a href="#cite_note-354">[344]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hazareesingh (2004) explores how Napoleon's image and memory are best understood. They played a key role in collective political defiance of the Bourbon restoration monarchy in 1815–1830. People from different walks of life and areas of France, particularly Napoleonic veterans, drew on the Napoleonic legacy and its connections with the ideals of the 1789 Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_355-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-355">[345]</a></sup>
</p><p>Widespread rumours of Napoleon's return from St. Helena and Napoleon as an inspiration for <a href="/wiki/Patriotism" title="Patriotism">patriotism</a>, individual and collective liberties, and political mobilization manifested themselves in seditious materials, displaying the tricolor and rosettes. There were also subversive activities celebrating anniversaries of Napoleon's life and reign and disrupting royal celebrations—they demonstrated the prevailing and successful goal of the varied supporters of Napoleon to constantly destabilize the Bourbon regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_355-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-355">[345]</a></sup>
</p><p>Datta (2005) shows that, following the collapse of militaristic <a href="/wiki/Georges_Ernest_Boulanger" title="Georges Ernest Boulanger">Boulangism</a> in the late 1880s, the Napoleonic legend was divorced from party politics and revived in popular culture. Concentrating on two plays and two novels from the period—<a href="/wiki/Victorien_Sardou" title="Victorien Sardou">Victorien Sardou</a>'s <i>Madame Sans-Gêne</i> (1893), <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Barr%C3%A8s" title="Maurice Barrès">Maurice Barrès</a>'s <i>Les Déracinés</i> (1897), <a href="/wiki/Edmond_Rostand" title="Edmond Rostand">Edmond Rostand</a>'s <i>L'Aiglon</i> (1900), and <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_de_Lorde" title="André de Lorde">André de Lorde</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sibylle_Gabrielle_Marie_Antoinette_Riqueti_de_Mirabeau" title="Sibylle Gabrielle Marie Antoinette Riqueti de Mirabeau">Gyp</a>'s <i>Napoléonette</i> (1913)—Datta examines how writers and critics of the <i><a href="/wiki/Belle_%C3%89poque" title="Belle Époque">Belle Époque</a></i> exploited the Napoleonic legend for diverse political and cultural ends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_356-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-356">[346]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reduced to a minor character, the new fictional Napoleon became not a world historical figure but an intimate one, fashioned by individuals' needs and consumed as popular entertainment. In their attempts to represent the emperor as a figure of national unity, proponents and detractors of the Third Republic used the legend as a vehicle for exploring anxieties about gender and fears about the processes of democratization that accompanied this new era of mass politics and culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_356-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-356">[346]</a></sup>
</p><p>International Napoleonic Congresses take place regularly, with participation by members of the French and American military, French politicians and scholars from different countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-357"><a href="#cite_note-357">[347]</a></sup> In January 2012, the <a href="/wiki/Mayor_(France)" title="Mayor (France)">mayor</a> of <a href="/wiki/Montereau-Fault-Yonne" title="Montereau-Fault-Yonne">Montereau-Fault-Yonne</a>, near Paris—the site of a <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Montereau" title="Battle of Montereau">late victory</a> of Napoleon—proposed development of <a href="/wiki/Napoleonland" title="Napoleonland">Napoleon's Bivouac</a>, a commemorative theme park at a projected cost of 200 million euros.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-358"><a href="#cite_note-358">[348]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">

</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Long-term_influence_outside_France">Long-term influence outside France</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Influence_of_the_French_Revolution" title="Influence of the French Revolution">Influence of the French Revolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_USCapitol_-_Napoleon_I_(1769-1821).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2379" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Flickr_-_USCapitol_-_Napoleon_I_%281769-1821%29.jpg/170px-Flickr_-_USCapitol_-_Napoleon_I_%281769-1821%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Flickr_-_USCapitol_-_Napoleon_I_%281769-1821%29.jpg/255px-Flickr_-_USCapitol_-_Napoleon_I_%281769-1821%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Flickr_-_USCapitol_-_Napoleon_I_%281769-1821%29.jpg/340px-Flickr_-_USCapitol_-_Napoleon_I_%281769-1821%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_USCapitol_-_Napoleon_I_(1769-1821).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Bas-reliefs_in_the_chamber_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="commons:Bas-reliefs in the chamber of the United States House of Representatives">Bas-relief</a> of Napoleon in the chamber of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a></div></div></div>
<p>Napoleon was responsible for spreading the values of the French Revolution to other countries, especially in <a href="/wiki/Law_reform" title="Law reform">legal reform</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-359"><a href="#cite_note-359">[349]</a></sup> After the fall of Napoleon, not only was it retained by conquered countries including the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, parts of Italy and Germany, but it has been used as the basis of certain parts of law outside Europe including the Dominican Republic, the US state of Louisiana and the Canadian province of Quebec.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-360"><a href="#cite_note-360">[350]</a></sup> The code was also used as a model in many parts of <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-361"><a href="#cite_note-361">[351]</a></sup> The reputation of Napoleon in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> has been favourable, especially for his support of <a href="/wiki/Independence" title="Independence">independence</a>, opposition to Russia, his legal code, the <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_abolition_of_slavery_and_serfdom" title="Timeline of abolition of slavery and serfdom">abolition of serfdom</a>, and the introduction of modern <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle class</a> administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-362"><a href="#cite_note-362">[352]</a></sup>
</p><p>Napoleon had an influence on the establishment of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Germany#1815–1871" title="History of Germany">modern Germany</a>. He caused the end of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> and helped create middle sized states such as Bavaria and Württemberg along the great powers Prussia and Austria. Although he also directly or indirectly helped to reduce the number of German states (from about 300 to fewer than 50), the middle sized states tried to prevent <a href="/wiki/Unification_of_Germany" title="Unification of Germany">the unification of Germany as a federalist state</a>. A byproduct of the French occupation was a strong development in <a href="/wiki/German_nationalism" title="German nationalism">German nationalism</a> which eventually turned the <a href="/wiki/German_Confederation" title="German Confederation">German Confederation</a> into the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> after a series of conflicts and other political developments.
</p><p>Napoleon indirectly began the process of <a href="/wiki/Spanish_American_wars_of_independence" title="Spanish American wars of independence">Latin American independence</a> when he invaded <a href="/wiki/Peninsular_War" title="Peninsular War">Spain</a> in 1808. The abdication of King <a href="/wiki/Charles_IV_of_Spain" title="Charles IV of Spain">Charles IV</a> and renunciation of his son, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferdinand_VII" title="Ferdinand VII">Ferdinand VII</a> created a power vacuum that was filled by <a href="/wiki/Criollo_people" title="Criollo people">native born</a> political leaders such as <a href="/wiki/Sim%C3%B3n_Bol%C3%ADvar" title="Simón Bolívar">Simón Bolívar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_de_San_Mart%C3%ADn" title="José de San Martín">José de San Martín</a>. Such leaders embraced nationalistic sentiments influenced by French nationalism and led successful independence movements in Latin America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-363"><a href="#cite_note-363">[353]</a></sup>
</p><p>Napoleon also significantly aided the United States when he agreed to <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">sell</a> the territory of <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> for 15 million dollars during the presidency of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>. That territory almost doubled the size of the United States, adding the equivalent of 13 states to the Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGraw-Hill_306-1"><a href="#cite_note-McGraw-Hill-306">[296]</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1796 to 2020, at least 95 major ships were named for him. In the 21st century, at least 18 Napoleon ships are operated under the flag of France, as well as Indonesia, Germany, Italy, Australia, Argentina, India, Netherlands, and the United Kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-364"><a href="#cite_note-364">[354]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Wives.2C_mistresses.2C_and_children"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wives,_mistresses,_and_children">Wives, mistresses, and children</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Le_divorce_de_l%27Imp%C3%A9ratrice_Jos%C3%A9phine_15_d%C3%A9cembre_1809_(Henri-Frederic_Schopin).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="974" data-file-width="1400" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Le_divorce_de_l%27Imp%C3%A9ratrice_Jos%C3%A9phine_15_d%C3%A9cembre_1809_%28Henri-Frederic_Schopin%29.jpg/220px-Le_divorce_de_l%27Imp%C3%A9ratrice_Jos%C3%A9phine_15_d%C3%A9cembre_1809_%28Henri-Frederic_Schopin%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Le_divorce_de_l%27Imp%C3%A9ratrice_Jos%C3%A9phine_15_d%C3%A9cembre_1809_%28Henri-Frederic_Schopin%29.jpg/330px-Le_divorce_de_l%27Imp%C3%A9ratrice_Jos%C3%A9phine_15_d%C3%A9cembre_1809_%28Henri-Frederic_Schopin%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Le_divorce_de_l%27Imp%C3%A9ratrice_Jos%C3%A9phine_15_d%C3%A9cembre_1809_%28Henri-Frederic_Schopin%29.jpg/440px-Le_divorce_de_l%27Imp%C3%A9ratrice_Jos%C3%A9phine_15_d%C3%A9cembre_1809_%28Henri-Frederic_Schopin%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Le_divorce_de_l%27Imp%C3%A9ratrice_Jos%C3%A9phine_15_d%C3%A9cembre_1809_(Henri-Frederic_Schopin).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9phine_de_Beauharnais" title="Joséphine de Beauharnais">Joséphine</a>, first wife of Napoleon, obtained the civil dissolution of her marriage under the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Code" title="Napoleonic Code">Napoleonic Code</a>, painting by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Schopin" title="Henri Frédéric Schopin">Henri Frédéric Schopin</a>, 1843</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marriage_of_Napoleon_and_Marie-Louise.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="741" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Marriage_of_Napoleon_and_Marie-Louise.jpg/220px-Marriage_of_Napoleon_and_Marie-Louise.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Marriage_of_Napoleon_and_Marie-Louise.jpg/330px-Marriage_of_Napoleon_and_Marie-Louise.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Marriage_of_Napoleon_and_Marie-Louise.jpg/440px-Marriage_of_Napoleon_and_Marie-Louise.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marriage_of_Napoleon_and_Marie-Louise.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marriage of Napoleon and <a href="/wiki/Marie_Louise,_Duchess_of_Parma" title="Marie Louise, Duchess of Parma">Marie-Louise</a> by <a href="/wiki/Georges_Rouget" title="Georges Rouget">Georges Rouget</a>, 1843</div></div></div>
<p>Napoleon married <a href="/wiki/Empress_Jos%C3%A9phine" title="Empress Joséphine">Joséphine</a> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N%C3%A9e" title="Née">née</a></i> Marie Josèphe Rose Tascher de La Pagerie) in 1796, when he was 26; she was a 32-year-old widow whose first husband, <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_de_Beauharnais" title="Alexandre de Beauharnais">Alexandre de Beauharnais</a>, had been executed during the <a href="/wiki/Reign_of_Terror" title="Reign of Terror">Reign of Terror</a>. Five days after Alexandre de Beauharnais' death, the Reign of Terror initiator <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maximilien_de_Robespierre" title="Maximilien de Robespierre">Maximilien de Robespierre</a> <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Maximilien_Robespierre" title="Fall of Maximilien Robespierre">was overthrown and executed</a>, and, with the help of high-placed friends, Joséphine was freed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-365"><a href="#cite_note-365">[355]</a></sup> Until she met Bonaparte, she had been known as "Rose", a name which he disliked. He called her "Joséphine" instead, and she went by this name henceforth. Bonaparte often sent her love letters while on his campaigns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-366"><a href="#cite_note-366">[356]</a></sup> He formally adopted her son <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_de_Beauharnais" title="Eugène de Beauharnais">Eugène</a> and second cousin (via marriage) <a href="/wiki/St%C3%A9phanie_de_Beauharnais" title="Stéphanie de Beauharnais">Stéphanie</a> and arranged dynastic marriages for them. Joséphine had her daughter <a href="/wiki/Hortense_de_Beauharnais" title="Hortense de Beauharnais">Hortense</a> marry Napoleon's brother <a href="/wiki/Louis_Bonaparte" title="Louis Bonaparte">Louis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-367"><a href="#cite_note-367">[357]</a></sup>
</p><p>Joséphine had lovers, such as <a href="/wiki/Hippolyte_Charles" title="Hippolyte Charles">Lieutenant Hippolyte Charles</a>, during Napoleon's Italian campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-368"><a href="#cite_note-368">[358]</a></sup> Napoleon learnt of that affair and a letter he wrote about it was intercepted by the British and published widely, to embarrass Napoleon. Napoleon had his own affairs too: during the Egyptian campaign he took <a href="/wiki/Pauline_Four%C3%A8s" title="Pauline Fourès">Pauline Bellisle Fourès</a>, the wife of a junior officer, as his mistress. She became known as "Cleopatra".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-370"><a href="#cite_note-370">[k]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-371"><a href="#cite_note-371">[360]</a></sup>
</p><p>While Napoleon's mistresses had children by him, Joséphine did not produce an heir, possibly because of either the stresses of her imprisonment during the Reign of Terror or an abortion she may have had in her twenties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-372"><a href="#cite_note-372">[361]</a></sup> Napoleon chose divorce so he could remarry in search of an heir. Despite his divorce from Josephine, Napoleon showed his dedication to her for the rest of his life. When he heard the news of her death while in exile in Elba, he locked himself in his room and would not come out for two full days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs.org_216-1"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-216">[208]</a></sup> Her name would also be his final word on his deathbed in 1821.
</p><p>On 11 March 1810 by <a href="/wiki/Proxy_marriage" title="Proxy marriage">proxy</a>, he married the 19-year-old <a href="/wiki/Marie_Louise,_Duchess_of_Parma" title="Marie Louise, Duchess of Parma">Marie Louise</a>, Archduchess of Austria, and a great-niece of <a href="/wiki/Marie_Antoinette" title="Marie Antoinette">Marie Antoinette</a>. Thus he had married into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Family_tree_of_the_German_monarchs" title="Family tree of the German monarchs">German royal and imperial family</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLynn_1998,_p.663_373-0"><a href="#cite_note-McLynn_1998,_p.663-373">[362]</a></sup> Louise was less than happy with the arrangement, at least at first, stating: "Just to see the man would be the worst form of torture". Her great-aunt had been executed in France, while Napoleon had fought numerous campaigns against Austria all throughout his military career. However, she seemed to warm up to him over time. After her wedding, she wrote to her father: "He loves me very much. I respond to his love sincerely. There is something very fetching and very eager about him that is impossible to resist".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs.org_216-2"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-216">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p>Napoleon and Marie Louise remained married until his death, though she did not join him in exile on Elba and thereafter never saw her husband again. The couple had one child, <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_II" title="Napoleon II">Napoleon Francis Joseph Charles</a> (1811–1832), known from birth as the <a href="/wiki/King_of_the_Romans" title="King of the Romans">King of Rome</a>. He became Napoleon II in 1814 and reigned for only two weeks. He was awarded the title of the Duke of Reichstadt in 1818 and died of <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> aged 21, with no children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLynn_1998,_p.663_373-1"><a href="#cite_note-McLynn_1998,_p.663-373">[362]</a></sup>
</p><p>Napoleon acknowledged one illegitimate son: <a href="/wiki/Charles_L%C3%A9on" title="Charles Léon">Charles Léon</a> (1806–1881) by <a href="/wiki/El%C3%A9onore_Denuelle_de_La_Plaigne" title="Eléonore Denuelle de La Plaigne">Eléonore Denuelle de La Plaigne</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m630_374-0"><a href="#cite_note-m630-374">[363]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Colonna-Walewski" title="Alexandre Colonna-Walewski">Alexandre Colonna-Walewski</a> (1810–1868), the son of his mistress <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maria_Walewska" title="Maria Walewska">Maria Walewska</a>, although acknowledged by Walewska's husband, was also widely known to be his child, and the DNA of his direct male descendant has been used to help confirm Napoleon's <a href="/wiki/Haplotype#Y-DNA_haplotypes_from_genealogical_DNA_tests" title="Haplotype">Y-chromosome haplotype</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-375"><a href="#cite_note-375">[364]</a></sup> He may have had further unacknowledged illegitimate offspring as well, such as Eugen Megerle von Mühlfeld by Emilie Victoria Kraus von Wolfsberg<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLynn423_376-0"><a href="#cite_note-McLynn423-376">[365]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A9l%C3%A8ne_Napoleone_Bonaparte" title="Hélène Napoleone Bonaparte">Hélène Napoleone Bonaparte</a> (1816–1907) by <a href="/wiki/Albine_de_Montholon" title="Albine de Montholon">Albine de Montholon</a>.
</p>

</li>
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">Napoléon I<sup>er</sup></i></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">He established a system of public education,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrab200356_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrab200356-9">[7]</a></sup> abolished the vestiges of <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broers_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Broers-10">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_and_the_Jews" title="Napoleon and the Jews">emancipated Jews</a> and other religious minorities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conner_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Conner-11">[9]</a></sup> abolished the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Inquisition" title="Spanish Inquisition">Spanish Inquisition</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JosephPerez_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-JosephPerez-12">[10]</a></sup> enacted <a href="/wiki/Equality_before_the_law" title="Equality before the law">legal protections</a> for an emerging middle class,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FisherBarnes_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FisherBarnes-13">[11]</a></sup> and centralized state power at the expense of religious authorities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">His brother, also called Napoleon, died at birth and his sister, Maria Anna, died shortly before her first birthday. In total, two siblings died at birth and three died in infancy.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles_(1768)" title="Treaty of Versailles (1768)">1768 Treaty of Versailles</a> formally ceded Corsica's rights, it remained un-incorporated during 1769<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-21">[17]</a></sup> until it became a <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_France" title="Provinces of France">province of France</a> in 1770.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[18]</a></sup> Corsica would be legally integrated as a <a href="/wiki/Departments_of_France" title="Departments of France">département</a> in 1789.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoberts2014142_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts2014142-24">[20]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Aside from his name, there does not appear to be a connection between him and <a href="/wiki/Napoleon%27s_theorem" title="Napoleon's theorem">Napoleon's theorem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[34]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">He was mainly referred to as Bonaparte until he became First Consul for life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m290_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-m290-45">[39]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-105"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-105">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This is depicted in <i><a href="/wiki/Bonaparte_Crossing_the_Alps" title="Bonaparte Crossing the Alps">Bonaparte Crossing the Alps</a></i> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hippolyte_Delaroche" title="Hippolyte Delaroche">Hippolyte Delaroche</a> and in Jacques-Louis David's imperial <i>Napoleon Crossing the Alps</i>. He is less realistically portrayed on a <a href="/wiki/Horses_in_warfare" title="Horses in warfare">charger</a> in the latter work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[97]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-243"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-243">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It was customary to cast a death mask of a leader. At least four genuine death masks of Napoleon are known to exist: one in <a href="/wiki/The_Cabildo" title="The Cabildo">The Cabildo</a> in New Orleans, one in a Liverpool museum, another in Havana and one in the library of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_North_Carolina" title="University of North Carolina">University of North Carolina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[234]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-251"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-251">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The body can tolerate large doses of arsenic if ingested regularly, and arsenic was a fashionable <a href="/wiki/Panacea_(medicine)" title="Panacea (medicine)">cure-all</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[241]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-370"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-370">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">One night, during an illicit liaison with actress <a href="/wiki/Marguerite_Georges" title="Marguerite Georges">Marguerite Georges</a>, Napoleon had a major fit. This and other more minor attacks have led historians to debate whether he had epilepsy and, if so, to what extent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-369"><a href="#cite_note-369">[359]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

"It is a historical cliché to compare the Schlieffen Plan with <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">Hannibal</a>'s tactical envelopment at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cannae" title="Battle of Cannae">Cannae</a> (216 BC); Schlieffen owed more to Napoleon's strategic maneuver on Ulm (1805)".</span>
